SECTION 1.  
PROJECT CONSIDERATIONS 
The existing Sand Island Wastewater Treatment Plant (WWTP) is located along Sand 
Island Parkway in the Sand Island area of Honolulu on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii.  The project 
location and general vicinity are shown on the Project Location Map, Plate 1.  Based on the 
information provided, we understand that it is desired to expand the secondary treatment 
facility of the WWTP along the east, south, and west sides of the wastewater treatment plant.  
A design-level field exploration and laboratory testing program was undertaken to assist the 
design team in the detailed design of the project. 
A summary of the field exploration program conducted for the project is presented 
below. Detailed information and discussions for each specific work item are provided in the 
respective appendices of this geotechnical data report.

SECTION 2.  
SCOPE OF WORK 
The purpose of our field exploration and laboratory testing program is to obtain an 
overview of the surface and subsurface conditions at the Sand Island WWTP site in support of 
the design of the Secondary Treatment – Phase 1 project. 
Our work was performed in general accordance with the scope of services outlined in 
our fee proposals dated October 13, 2016 and January 3, 2018, and our Geotechnical 
Investigation Work Plan dated February 1, 2018.  In order to accomplish this, we conducted a 
field exploration and laboratory testing program consisting of the following tasks and efforts: 
1. 
Research and review of in-house geologic and soils information in the project 
vicinity. A list of the references used in the preparation of this geotechnical data 
report is presented in the reference section.  
2. 
Preparation and submittal of the following project documents: 
Geotechnical Investigation Work Plan 
Accident Prevention Plan with Activity Hazard Analysis 
3. 
Site reconnaissance to review the boring locations and access considerations for 
drilling. 
4. 
Coordination with the Sand Island WWTP, Frank Coluccio Construction Company, 
and AECOM for the field exploration work. 
5. 
Stakeout of the proposed boring locations by our geologist. 
6. 
Coordination of utility toning and clearance of the boring locations with the 
Hawaii One-Call Center. 
7. 
Mobilization and demobilization of a truck-mounted drill rig, water truck, and 
operators to and from the project site. 
8. 
Drilling and sampling of seventeen (17) borings extending to depths ranging from 
about 5.5 to 103.5 feet below the existing ground/pavement surface for a total 
of about 1,008 lineal feet of exploration. 
9. 
Backfilling of the drilled borings with cement grout upon completion of the soil 
and/or rock sampling program, with the exception of the groundwater 
monitoring point locations. 
SECTION 2. SCOPE OF WORK 
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 5 
Hawaii  California 
10. 
Coordination of the field exploration and logging of the borings by our geologist. 
11. 
Performance of seismic shear wave velocity profiling using seismic cone 
penetration test (SCPT) equipment at one (1) selected boring location (B-5) to a 
depth of about 100.1 feet for seismic design considerations.  
12. 
Conversion of two (2) selected borings (B-3 and B-11) to groundwater level 
monitoring points with the installation of two vibrating wire piezometers with 
cement-bentonite grout and traffic rated hole covers over the monitoring points. 
13. 
Monitoring of the groundwater levels in the groundwater monitoring points 
utilizing dataloggers with the data transmitted via a wireless system for 
processing. 
14. 
Performance of a total of eight (8) permeability tests with one constant head and 
one falling head tests at each of the four selected boring locations (B-3, B-5, B-6, 
and B-7) to evaluate the permeability characteristics of the subsurface materials 
for dewatering evaluation purpose. 
15. 
Performance of infiltration tests at four selected locations to evaluate the 
infiltration characteristics of the subsurface materials in support of the design of 
the storm water runoff disposal system. 
16. 
Laboratory testing of selected soil and core samples retrieved from our field 
exploration as an aid in classifying the materials and evaluating their engineering 
properties. 
17. 
Preparation of this geotechnical data report presenting the results of the 
geotechnical data review, subsurface explorations, groundwater observations, 
and laboratory tests. 
18. 
Coordination of our overall work on the project by our senior engineer. 
19. 
Quality assurance of our work on the project by our principal engineer. 
20. 
Miscellaneous work efforts such as drafting, word processing, and clerical 
support.

SECTION 3.  
SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
3.1 
Regional Geology 
The Island of Oahu is composed largely of the weathered remnants of two extinct shield 
volcanoes - Waianae and Koolau.  The older Waianae Volcano forms the bulk of the western 
one-third of the island while the younger Koolau Volcano forms the majority of the eastern 
two-thirds of the island.  The Waianae Volcano became extinct while the Koolau Volcano was 
still active, and its eastern flank is partially buried below the Koolau Volcano lavas in Central 
Oahu. 
The Koolau Volcanic Shield was built during the early Pleistocene Epoch by 
thinly-bedded basaltic lava flows.  The main shield-building activities ceased approximately 
2.5 million years ago.  Evidence from drilled wells indicates that the Island of Oahu has subsided 
by as much as 1,200 feet since the cessation of this early volcanic activity (Macdonald and 
Abbott, 1970). 
During that period of subsidence, coral-algal reefs began to grow on the southern coast 
of the Island of Oahu forming broad bays with barrier reefs across its mouth.  The growth of the 
reefs essentially kept pace with the rate of subsidence.  A series of lagoons formed behind the 
barrier reefs and both terrigenous and marine sediments accumulated in the lagoons 
(Macdonald and Abbott, 1970). 
During the later Pleistocene Epoch (Ice Age), many sea level changes occurred as a result 
of widespread glaciation in the continental areas of the world.  As the great continental glaciers 
advanced, the level of the ocean fell since there was less water available to fill the oceanic 
basins.  Conversely, as the glaciers receded or melted, global sea levels rose because more 
water was available.  The land mass of Oahu remained essentially stable during these changes, 
and the fluctuations were eustatic in nature.  These glacio-eustatic fluctuations resulted in 
stands of the sea that were both higher and lower relative to the present sea level of Oahu. 
The higher sea level stands caused the accumulation of deltas and fans of terrigenous 
sediments in the heads of old bays, accumulation of reef deposits at correspondingly higher 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 7 
Hawaii  California 
elevations, and deposition of lagoonal/marine sediments in the quiet waters protected by 
fringing reefs.  The lower sea stands caused streams to carve valleys in the sediments and reef 
deposits.  Subaerial exposure of the sediments and calcareous materials caused desiccation of 
the soft deltaic materials and lagoonal deposits and induration of the calcareous reef materials.  
In addition, renewed subaerial erosion of the upper areas of the volcanic dome deposited 
terrigenous alluvial soils under relatively high-energy conditions. 
During periods of no significant sea level changes, continued stream action extended 
the alluvial deltas and fans seaward and deposited alluvium over the lagoonal sediments.  
During these stable periods, the growth of fringing coral/algal reefs formed distinct ledges of 
reef limestone at elevations roughly conforming to the level of the sea during that period.  
About 15,000 years ago, a relatively rapid rise in sea level occurred.  During that rise, the valleys 
in the project area were submerged.  In the last 10,000 years or so, the sea level has adjusted to 
its present stand.  Terrigenous and marine sediments have continued to accumulate in 
low-energy estuarine or lagoonal environments, resulting in thick deposits of soft and/or loose 
sediments along the coast in areas that were formerly valleys and drainage ways. 
The project site is located on the seaward fringe of the Honolulu Coastal Plain.  The 
coastal plain is composed of interbedded alluvial, marine, and post-erosional volcanic deposits.  
Extensive development of the plain and reclamation of the low-lying areas have given the land 
its present shape.  There is evidence that a basaltic lava flow of the Honolulu Volcanic Series, 
possibly originating from the Makuku vent in Nuuanu or from Punchbowl, reached the eastern 
portion of what is now Sand Island.  During the Waipio stand of the sea at Elevation -60 feet 
Mean Sea Level (MSL), this layer of basalt has provided a resistant surface, which has diverted 
the flows of Nuuanu and Kapalama Streams to the west.  The diversion of these two streams 
has resulted in the deposition of extensive subaerial sediments consisting of alluvial clays and 
silts.  These exposed alluvial deposits have become consolidated due to seasonal moisture 
changes. 
Subsequently, as the sea level rose to the Waimanalo stand of the sea at about 
Elevation +25 feet MSL and then fell to its present level, the stream-laid alluvium was buried 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 8 
Hawaii  California 
beneath backreef deposits of sands as a fringing coral reef grew and emerged on the seaward 
margin of the island.  Land reclamation projects during this century have built Sand Island to its 
current topography.  Many of the resulting fills at the ground surface are of poor quality in 
terms of supporting large structural loads. 
3.2 
Site Description 
The Sand Island WWTP is located along Sand Island Parkway in the Sand Island area on 
the southern shore of the Island of Oahu, Hawaii.  Sand Island is bounded by Honolulu Harbor 
to the northwest through the southeast and the Pacific Ocean to the south as shown on the 
Project Location Map, Plate 1.  Sand Island is comprised of reclaimed land built from coralline 
and marine earth materials dredged from Honolulu Harbor and the adjacent Keehi Lagoon.  
These dredged materials were placed on top of a submerged coral reef platform. 
Based on the preliminary drawings provided, the proposed secondary treatment facility 
expansion is generally located along the east, south, and west sides of the WWTP. Our field 
observations indicated that the majority of the project area is occupied by the Frank Coluccio 
Construction Company, Department of Facilities Maintenance, Drug Treatment Center, open 
detention area, embankment fill with fabric wall, open space, and other structures.  Based on 
the information provided, the project site is relatively flat with existing ground surface 
elevations generally ranging between about +5 and +10 feet MSL with the exception of the 
embankment fill, which ranges from about +15 to +17 feet MSL. 
3.3 
Subsurface Characteristics 
In general, we observed four main stratigraphic units across the project area within the 
Sand Island WWTP facility. The stratigraphic units consist of soil and rock materials of varying 
character and engineering properties. The four main stratigraphic units are listed below in the 
general order of geologic age, beginning with the youngest unit and ending with the oldest unit: 
1. 
Recent Fill (Rf) 
2. 
Recent Alluvium (Ra) 
3. 
Coral Limestone (Qls) 
4. 
Older Alluvium (Qa) 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 9 
Hawaii  California 
The following sections briefly describe the general characteristics of each of the above 
stratigraphic units based on our literature research, past experience with similar materials, and 
the field exploration and laboratory testing program conducted at selected locations across the 
project site. The approximate delineation of some of these stratigraphic units at the ground 
surface of the project vicinity is presented on the Geology Map, Plate 3. 
3.3.1 Recent Fill (Rf) 
This stratigraphic unit represents low lying areas that were filled during urbanization. 
The majority of these low lying areas is underlain at relatively shallow depths by recent 
alluvium and was originally marsh areas. Small parts of the filled lands along the section 
alignment are underlain by older alluvial soil deposits. In areas underlain by the recent 
alluvium, the fill materials generally consist of clayey silts and sandy silts with stiff to 
hard consistencies. 
The quality and composition of the fill materials are variable. These fills were frequently 
placed without proper compaction control. Therefore, it is difficult to make any general 
statement regarding their engineering properties or construction in these areas. 
3.3.2 Recent Alluvium (Ra) 
These soils generally are the result of sedimentation in a very low energy environment 
such as an estuary or bay. Frequently, the soils assigned to this unit accumulated very 
rapidly under water and might not have been exposed to air. Therefore, there is a 
tendency to encounter under-consolidated [OCR (Over Consolidation Ratio) < 1.0] 
sediments, which settle under the weight of minimal overburden or structural loading. 
Recent alluvium generally is found within some stream channels and bordering some 
stream banks, at the mouths (outlets) of streams, and within broad valley floors and 
basin features surrounded by hills. These soils also may be found in proximity to swamp 
and tidal flat environments. The following subunits are used in this geotechnical data 
report. 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 7384-10

Page 10 
Hawaii  California 
Recent Alluvium 
This subunit generally consists of tan to brown silty clays with varying amounts 
of sands and infrequent pockets of gravel and cobbles. The consistency is 
generally in the range of very soft to medium stiff. 
Lagoonal Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of light gray to medium gray sandy silts or silty 
sands in a very soft to soft consistency and/or very loose to loose relative 
density. Gravel-sized fragments are rare. Frequently, the silts are organic in 
origin and may have a mild to strong organic odor. 
Marsh Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of dark gray and brown silts and clays with fibrous 
organic materials in a very soft to soft consistency. This soil unit contains traces 
of fine to medium grained sands. They are characterized by a moderate to strong 
organic odor and may contain small fragile mollusk shells. 
3.3.3 Coral Limestone (Qls) 
This unit consists of fossil remnants of coral-algal reefs and their related detrital debris 
deposits. In Hawaii, the detrital deposits tend to be the dominant portion of any 
coralline rock assemblage. The detrital deposits are weakly to strongly cemented 
coralline debris consisting of gravel, sand, and silt-sized clastic fragments. Some of the 
coralline fragments were dissolved, and the solution cavities were filled with cemented 
and/or uncemented fine sands and silts. The following subunits are used in this 
geotechnical data report. 
Coralline Detritus 
This subunit consists of granular detritus broken from reef formations. The soils 
generally consist of medium dense to dense sandy gravel and gravelly sands. Silts 
and clays are only present in minor or trace amounts. Detritus also may be 
weakly cemented with occasional moderate to strong cementation. 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 11 
Hawaii  California 
Coral Formation 
This subunit is composed of coral and calcareous algae in their original growth 
positions or detrital deposits, which have become moderately to well-cemented. 
Sandstone 
Sandstone is a sedimentary rock composed mainly of cemented sand-sized 
particles (clasts) consisting of individual mineral particles or grains of weathered 
parent rock. The sedimentary particles comprising sandstone typically are 
derived from the breakdown of parent material into sedimentary particles. The 
sedimentary particles typically are eroded, transported and sorted by water or 
air, and deposited as layers of sedimentary materials. Over time, the sediments 
may be buried by other sediments or geologic layers, and the burial pressure 
combined with a cementing agent act to consolidate the grains and cement the 
pore space between the grains to form hardened sandstone. Typical cementing 
agents may be calcium carbonate (common in calcareous sand deposits of 
Hawaii) or silica and some clays.  
Most sandstones in Hawaii are light brown in color and calcareous being derived 
from the breakdown of parent coralline and reef materials into sand-sized 
particles. The cementing agent in most calcareous sandstone is calcium 
carbonate. Inclusions of basaltic rock fragments, marine shells, and other 
fragments of broken coral may be cemented in the sandstone layers. Sandstones 
containing such clast inclusions commonly are referred to as beachrock in 
Hawaii, having been derived from available shoreline geologic materials.  
The strength (hardness) of sandstone generally depends on the grain size, 
degree of sorting, and degree of cementation of the sedimentary materials. The 
weathering and decomposition of calcareous sandstone may be accelerated by 
exposure to acidic water, which attacks the calcareous cement binding the sand 
grains and causes a slow exfoliation of the outermost exposed layers of the 
sandstone outcropping. Sandstone may or may not contain visible bedding 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 12 
Hawaii  California 
surfaces. Aeolian (wind derived) sandstone may exhibit cross bedding structures 
that developed during the initial deposition of the sand layers. 
3.3.4 Older Alluvium (Qa) 
Older alluvial deposits are generally very stiff to hard silty and clayey soils that originally 
were deposited in alluvial environments, buried over long periods of time by more 
recent deposits to become consolidated, and then subsequently uncovered at the 
ground surface by erosion or other geologic processes. This unit generally overlies the 
Koolau Basalt and consists of terrigenous sediments, which have been transported by 
stream action from the upper reaches of the streams.  
Gravel lenses representing buried stream channels sometimes are encountered. 
Portions of the Older Alluvium unit contain some weathered basaltic cobbles and 
boulders. These coarse-grained parts of the unit also may contain artesian groundwater 
from the basal fresh water system or as a sub-aquifer. The deposits may resemble 
saprolite soil deposits, but they are clearly differentiable by the layered characteristics.  
Frequently, these materials show a moderate to high shrink/swell potential when 
subjected to fluctuations in moisture. The following subunits are used in this 
geotechnical data report. 
Older Alluvium 
This subunit generally consists of tan to brown silty clays with varying amounts 
of sands and infrequent pockets of gravel and cobbles. The consistency is 
generally in the range of very stiff to hard.  
Old Lagoonal Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of light gray to medium gray sandy silts or silty 
sands in stiff to very stiff consistency and/or medium dense to dense relative 
density with various degrees of cementation. In some cases, strongly cemented 
deposits form siltstones. Gravel sized fragments are rare. Frequently, the silts are 
organic in origin and may have a mild to strong organic odor.  
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 13 
Hawaii  California 
3.3.5 Groundwater 
Groundwater level measurements were taken during advancement of the boreholes 
drilled for the project during our field exploration program. We encountered 
groundwater level in the drilled borings at depths ranging from about 4.0 to 16.8 feet 
below the existing ground and/or pavement surface. The groundwater levels measured 
generally correspond to about Elevations -1.2 to +2.4 feet MSL. The water level 
measurements collected are shown on the Logs of Borings and summarized in Tables 
C-1 through C-1.3 of Appendix C. It should be noted that groundwater levels are subject 
to change due to rainfall, time of year, seasonal precipitation, surface water runoff, and 
other factors. 
We explored the subsurface conditions across the project site by drilling and sampling 
seventeen borings, designated as Boring Nos. 1 through 13 and I-1 through I-4, extending to 
depths ranging from about 5.5 to 103.5 feet below the existing ground surface.  In addition, 
Boring Nos. 3 and 11 were converted into monitoring wells for groundwater level monitoring.  
Five bulk samples of the near-surface soils, designated as Bulk-1 through Bulk-5, were obtained 
to evaluate the pavement support characteristics of the near-surface soils. The approximate 
boring and bulk sample locations are shown on the Boring Location Plan, Plate 2. 
Based on our field exploration and available information, the project site generally is 
underlain by a surface fill layer, consisting of medium dense silty sands and stiff clayey silts, 
extending to depths of about 3 to 9 feet below the existing ground surface.  Below the 
near-surface fill layer, lagoonal deposits consisting of loose to very loose silty sands and gravel 
and soft sandy and gravelly clays were encountered to depths of about 23 to 40 feet below the 
existing ground surface.  Below the compressible lagoonal deposits, medium hard to hard coral 
and sandstone formation interbedded with medium dense coralline detritus were encountered 
and extended to the maximum depth explored of approximately 103.5 feet below the existing 
ground surface. 
Detailed descriptions of our field exploration methodology and the Logs of Borings are 
presented in Appendix A. Results of the laboratory tests performed on selected soil samples

W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 14 
Hawaii  California 
and rock cores are presented in Appendix F. Photographs of the rock cores are presented in 
Appendix G.

SECTION 4.  
GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM 
The geotechnical exploration program conducted for the Sand Island Wastewater 
Treatment Plant, Secondary Treatment – Phase 1 project generally consisted of the following 
field and laboratory work components. A brief description of each work component is 
presented below. Detailed discussions, findings, and test results of the various components are 
provided in the respective appendices herein. 
4.1 
Soil Borings, Rock Coring, and Associated Field Testing 
Soil and rock sampling methods and field testing were performed in accordance with 
the scope of services outlined in our fee proposals dated October 13, 2016 and January 3, 2018, 
and our Geotechnical Investigation Work Plan (GIWP) dated February 1, 2018. These field 
explorations are described in more detail in the approved GIWP, and in the introduction to 
Appendix A. The following discussion provides an overview of the methods and procedures. 
Typically, soil borings were advanced with 4-inch diameter continuous-flight solid-stem 
augers (SSA) to the first encounter with groundwater and then continued with PQ-size wireline 
coring methods to advance the PQ-size casing from the ground surface. In general, auger 
drilling was used in areas where stiff to hard cohesive soils (older alluvium) exist and in 
locations where rock coring was not anticipated. Additionally, SSA drilling was switched over to 
the wireline coring method when hard clays, boulders, and/or rock formation were 
encountered or when soft and/or loose soils that caved in were encountered. 
Sampling of the soils was performed at about 5-foot intervals. Drive samples were 
obtained using the modified California sampler (ASTM D3550) and the Standard Penetration 
Test (SPT) split-spoon sampler (ASTM D1586). In the softer soils, relatively “undisturbed” 
samples were obtained by driving a ring-lined modified California sampler (ASTM D3550) into 
the soil formation.  
Where the borehole was advanced utilizing the PQ core barrel in the soil formations, 
samples of the soils recovered from the PQ core barrel also were retained in core boxes for 
visual observation. Intervals where the borehole was advanced utilizing the PQ casing only as a 
SECTION 4. GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 16 
Hawaii  California 
rotary wash drilling method, there were no soil samples collected. The borings were advanced 
into the rock formations with the PQ-size wireline coring methods. The PQ-size wireline coring 
methods produce rock core samples of 3.35 inches (85 mm) in diameter. 
In-situ testing of the subsurface materials in the borings included the SPT, which was 
performed in accordance with ASTM D1586. Standard Penetration Test (SPT) sampling provides 
a measure of the resistance or consistency of a soil and is reported as the uncorrected N-value, 
which is defined as the number of blows required to drive a standard split-spoon sampler 
12 inches with a 140-lb hammer, after 6 inches of initial seating. The penetration resistance of 
the modified California sampler was recorded in a similar manner, and the modified California 
sampler blows per 12 inches can be approximately related to the SPT N-value through 
correlation. Detailed discussion on the hammer efficiency calibration is provided in Appendix H. 
Rock core recovery (REC) and rock quality designation (RQD) were measured as 
described in Appendix A. ASTM D6032 - Standard Test Method for Determining Rock Quality 
Designation (RQD) of Rock Core states that “Core sizes from BQ to PQ with core diameters of 
36.5 mm (1.44 in.) to 85 mm (3.35 in.), respectively, are normally acceptable for measuring RQD 
as long as proper drilling techniques are used that do not cause excessive core breakage or 
poor recovery, or both. The NX-size (54.7 mm [2.16 in.]) and NQ-size (47.5 mm [1.87 in.]) are 
the optimal core sizes for measuring RQD. The RQD is also useful for large core diameters 
provided the core diameter is clearly stated.” The rock core photographs are included in 
Appendix G for information and reference. 
4.2 
Seismic Shear Wave Velocity Testing 
Seismic shear wave velocity profiling was performed at a completed borehole (Boring 
No. 5) using seismic cone penetration testing (SCPT) equipment. Procedures for the test are 
discussed in more detail in Appendix B. In brief, the completed borehole was tremie-filled with 
time-release bentonite pellets and bentonite chips in order to provide a medium through which 
the seismic cone tip could be pushed. The sleeve and tip resistance through the bentonite-filled 
borehole are not meaningful because the cone is pushed through the bentonite backfill; 
SECTION 4. GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 17 
Hawaii  California 
however, the seismic wave travels from the ground surface through the surrounding soils, 
allowing measurement of the average seismic shear wave velocity. 
The seismic cone tip contains a geophone sensor. At a succession of depths, the 
advancement of the cone tip was halted, a seismic pulse was generated at the ground surface 
by striking a loaded plank with a switched hammer, and the resulting seismic shear wave was 
detected at the geophone. The distance from the source and the wave arrival time allow for 
determination of the average shear wave velocity over a depth interval. The measured average 
seismic shear wave velocity logs are presented in Appendix B. 
4.3 
Piezometric Water Level Monitoring 
Depths to groundwater, where encountered, were measured and recorded during the 
exploration activities and summarized in Tables C-1.1 thru C-1.3. In addition, vibrating wire 
piezometers (Model 4500 manufactured by Geokon) were installed in two selected boring 
locations (Boring Nos. 3 and 11) to monitor piezometric water levels and their seasonal 
fluctuations.  Each borehole was backfilled with cement-bentonite grout with a traffic rated 
hole cover over the monitoring point.  Data are recorded on a near-continuous basis using a 
Model LC-2 datalogger manufactured by Geokon and transmitted via a wireless system for 
processing from the time of installation (April 2018) through a 12-month monitoring period.  
After termination of the monitoring period, the monitoring well will be left functional unless 
otherwise directed by AECOM. Details of the piezometer installation and piezometric level 
readings are included in Appendix C. 
4.4 
Field Permeability Testing 
Field permeability tests (both constant head and falling head methods) were performed 
at depths of about 16.5 to 22 feet below the existing ground surface in selected borings (Boring 
Nos. 3, 5, 6, and 7) to obtain data pertaining to the dewatering operations anticipated for 
below-grade structures and underground piping.  Details of the permeability test procedures 
and the calculated hydraulic conductivities are provided in Appendix D.  
SECTION 4. GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 18 
Hawaii  California 
4.5 
Infiltration Testing 
In accordance with the City’s new storm water requirements, infiltration tests and test 
borings are required to support the on-site disposal design. To provide information for the 
design of the detention/filtration system, we advanced four boreholes (Boring Nos. I-1 through 
I-4) to depths of about 5.5 to 6.5 feet below the existing ground surface and installed 
temporary casing in the boreholes. The infiltration tests were conducted through the bottom of 
the cased boreholes in general accordance with the procedures described in the Maryland 
Stormwater Design Manual. 
Each boring was pre-soaked with water for approximately 2 hours prior to testing. 
Water was introduced into the boring to a depth of about 2 feet above the bottom of the 
boring; the drop of the water level in the boring was measured with respect to time for a falling 
head test consisting of four individual one hour trials per test. The water level was refilled, as 
necessary, for each trial.  
The tests were carried out in several increments until achieving a steady-state with a 
relatively constant water infiltration rate. Details of the test procedures and results of the final 
infiltration rates are provided in Appendix E.  
4.6 
Laboratory Testing 
Laboratory testing was performed on soil and rock samples to determine the physical 
and engineering properties. Laboratory tests performed included moisture content, unit 
weight, Atterberg Limits, grain size distribution, uniaxial compressive strength of rock, 
Unconsolidated-Undrained (UU) Triaxial Compression, Direct Shear, laboratory California 
Bearing Ratio (CBR), Modified Proctor, and corrosion potential tests (pH, minimum resistivity, 
chloride content, and sulfate content). The laboratory test results are provided in Appendix F. 
4.7 
Additional Subsurface Information 
Selected boring logs from previous explorations that are applicable to this project are 
included in Appendix I for information and reference purposes. 
END OF GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM 
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 19 
Hawaii  California 
SECTION 5.  
LIMITATIONS 
The geotechnical field and laboratory test data submitted in this geotechnical data 
report are based upon information obtained from the field exploration points. The locations 
and elevations of the field exploration points indicated in this report have been surveyed by 
AECOM Technical Services, Inc. following the completion of our field exploration program. 
The stratification lines shown on the graphic representations of the field exploration 
points depict the approximate boundaries between soil and rock types and, as such, may 
denote a gradual transition. Water level data presented on the logs of borings in this report 
were based on measurements recorded in the borings at the times shown on the graphic 
representations. It should be noted that fluctuation of the water level may occur due to 
variation in tides, rainfall, temperature, and other factors. 
This report has been prepared for the exclusive use of AECOM Technical Services, Inc. 
and their client, City and County of Honolulu – Department of Design and Construction, 
Wastewater Division, for specific application to the Sand Island Wastewater Treatment Plant, 
Secondary Treatment – Phase 1 project in accordance with generally accepted geotechnical 
engineering principles and practices. No warranty is expressed or implied.  
This geotechnical data report was not intended to investigate the potential presence of 
hazardous materials existing at the project site. It should be noted that the equipment, 
techniques, and personnel used to conduct a geo-environmental exploration differ substantially 
from those applied in geotechnical engineering.

APPENDIX A

A P P E N D I X   A 
Test Boring Exploration 
We explored the subsurface conditions across the project site by drilling and sampling 
17 borings, designated as Boring numbers B-1 through B-13 and I-1 through I-4. The borings 
extended to depths ranging from about 5.5 to 103.5 feet below the existing ground/pavement 
surface using a truck-mounted drill rig equipped with continuous-flight augers and rotary coring 
tools. The field exploration was conducted from March 26 through April 12, 2018. In addition, 
five bulk samples of the near-surface soils, designated as Bulk-1 through Bulk-5, were obtained 
for laboratory CBR and Modified Proctor tests.  The approximate boring and bulk sample 
locations are shown on the Boring Location Plan, Plate 2. 
Soil borings were advanced with 4-inch diameter continuous-flight solid-stem augers 
(SSA) to the first encounter with groundwater and then continued with PQ-size wireline coring 
methods to advance the PQ-size casing from the ground surface. In general, auger drilling was 
used in areas where stiff to hard cohesive soils (older alluvium) exist and in locations where 
rock coring is not anticipated. Additionally, SSA drilling was switched over to the wireline coring 
method when hard clays, boulders, and/or rock formation were encountered or when soft 
and/or loose soils that caved in were encountered.   After the borings were advanced to the 
maximum depth of the boreholes, the bored holes generally were backfilled with cement-grout 
with the exception of B-3 and B-11. 
Boring Nos. B-3(P) and B-11(P), which were converted to groundwater level monitoring 
points, were backfilled as depicted on Plates C-1.1 and C-2.1 of Appendix C. Boring No. B-5, 
selected for seismic shear wave velocity testing was backfilled with 0.25-inch diameter time-
release bentonite pellets (below the groundwater level) and bentonite chips (above the 
groundwater level). The temporary casing from the coring operations was used as a tremie pipe 
to place the time-release bentonite pellets starting from the bottom and advancing upward. 
Backfilling above the static groundwater table were performed in stages by adding water to 
limited amounts of bentonite chips to ensure hydration before additional bentonite chips were 
placed in the borehole. The top 3 feet of the borehole was then grouted with Quikcrete 
concrete mix. No additives were added to the drilling water during our drilling operations. 
Our geologist classified the materials encountered in the borings by visual and textural 
examination in the field in general accordance with ASTM D2488, Standard Practice for 
Description and Identification of Soils, and monitored the drilling operations on a 
near-continuous basis. These classifications were further reviewed visually and by testing in the 
laboratory. Soils were classified in general accordance with ASTM D2487, Standard Practice for 
Classification of Soils for Engineering Purposes (Unified Soil Classification System), as shown on 
the Soil Log Legend, Plate A-0.1. Deviations made to the soil classification in accordance with 
ASTM D2487 are described on the Soil Classification Log Key, Plate A-0.2. Graphic 
representations of the materials encountered and other observations, such as groundwater 
Appendix A 
Test Boring Exploration 
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
JUNE 2018     Page A-2 
Hawaii • California 
levels, artesian groundwater condition, drilling rate, etc. are presented on the Logs of Borings, 
Plates A-1 through A-17. 
Relatively “undisturbed” soil samples were obtained from the borings drilled in general 
accordance with ASTM D3550, Standard Practice for Thick Wall, Ring-Lined, Spilt Barrel, Drive 
Sampling of Soils, by driving a 3-inch OD modified California sampler with a 140-pound hammer 
falling 30 inches. In addition, some samples were obtained from the borings drilled in general 
accordance with ASTM D1586, Standard Test Method for Penetration Test and Split-Barrel 
Sampling of Soils, by driving a 2-inch OD standard penetration sampler using the same hammer 
and drop. The blow counts needed to drive the sampler the second and third 6 inches of an 
18-inch drive are shown as the “Penetration Resistance” on the Logs of Borings at the 
appropriate sample depths. 
Core samples of the rock materials encountered at the project site were obtained by 
using diamond core drilling techniques, consisting of a PQ wireline coring system with double 
tube core barrel (core size of 3.35 inches), in general accordance with ASTM D2113, Diamond 
Core Drilling for Site Investigation. Core drilling is a rotary drilling method that uses a hollow bit 
to cut into the rock formation. The rock material left in the hollow core of the bit is 
mechanically recovered for examination and description. The coring rates indicated on the Logs 
of Borings refer to the time required to complete that particular core run at that depth. Rock 
cores were described in general accordance with the Rock Description System, as shown on the 
Rock Log Legend, Plate A-0.3. The Rock Description System is based generally on the publication 
“Suggested Methods for the Quantitative Description of Discontinuities in Rock Masses” by 
International Society for Rock Mechanics (March 1977). 
Recovery (REC) is defined as the actual length of material recovered from a coring 
attempt versus the length of the core attempt. For example, if 3.7 feet of material is recovered 
from a 5.0-foot core run, the recovery would be 74 percent and would be shown on the Logs of 
Borings as REC = 74%. 
The Rock Quality Designation (RQD) is also a subjective guide to the relative quality of 
rock masses. RQD is defined as the percentage of the total core run in rock that is sound 
material in excess of 4 inches in length without any discontinuities, discounting any drilling, 
mechanical, and handling induced fractures or breaks. If 2.5 feet of sound material is recovered 
from a 5.0-foot core run in rock, the RQD would be 50 percent and would be shown on the Logs 
of Borings as RQD = 50%. Generally, the following is used to describe the relative quality of the 
rock based on the "Practical Handbook of Physical Properties of Rocks and Minerals” by Robert 
S. Carmichael (1989).

PRIMARY constituents are composed of the largest
percent of the soil mass. Primary constituents are
capitalized and bold (i.e., GRAVEL, SAND)
PRIMARY constituents are based on plasticity. Primary
constituents are capitalized and bold (i.e., CLAY, SILT)
SECONDARY constituents are composed of a
percentage less than the primary constituent. If the soil
mass consists of 12 percent or more fines content, a
cohesive constituent is used (SILTY or CLAYEY);
otherwise, a granular constituent is used (GRAVELLY
or SANDY) provided that the secondary constituent
consists of 20 percent or more of the soil mass.
Secondary constituents are capitalized and bold (i.e.,
SANDY GRAVEL, CLAYEY SAND) and precede the
primary constituent.
SECONDARY constituents are composed of a
percentage less than the primary constituent, but more
than 20 percent of the soil mass. Secondary constituents
are capitalized and bold (i.e., SANDY CLAY, SILTY
CLAY, CLAYEY SILT) and precede the primary
constituent.

Rock Log Legend
The following terms describe the resistance of a rock to indentation or scratching:
Specimen breaks with difficulty after several "pinging" hammer blows.
Example: Dense, fine grain volcanic rock
Specimen breaks with some difficulty after several hammer blows.
Example: Vesicular, vugular, coarse-grained rock
Specimen can be broked by one hammer blow. Cannot be scraped by knife. SPT may penetrate by
~25 blows per inch with bounce.
Example: Porous rock such as clinker, cinder, and coral reef
Can be indented by one hammer blow. Can be scraped or peeled by knife. SPT can penetrate by
~100 blows per foot.
Example: Weathered rock, chalk-like coral reef
Crumbles under hammer blow. Can be peeled and carved by knife. Can be indented by finger
pressure.
Example: Saprolite
CONGLOMERATE

A P P E N D I X   B 
Seismic Shear Wave Velocity Test 
Seismic shear wave velocity profiling of the subsurface materials was performed using 
Seismic Cone Penetration Testing (SCPT) equipment. Seismic shear wave velocity testing using 
seismic cone penetration testing equipment was performed at a selected boring location, 
designated as B-5(SW), as shown on the Boring Location Plan, Plate 2. 
The seismic shear wave velocity profiling was performed at approximately 6-foot 
intervals extending to a depth of about 100.1 feet below the existing ground surface. In order 
to conduct the seismic shear wave velocity test in the boring, the test boring generally was 
advanced utilizing rotary coring methods to the maximum depth of the boring. Logs of the 
materials encountered in the boring are presented on the Logs of Boring in Appendix A. After 
the boring was advanced to the maximum depth of the borehole, the bored hole was backfilled 
with 0.25-inch diameter time-release bentonite pellets. The temporary casing from the coring 
operations was used as a tremie pipe to place the bentonite pellets starting from the bottom 
and advancing upward. When the bentonite pellets are in contact with the groundwater in the 
borehole, the pellets started to hydrate slowly. As the bentonite pellets hydrate, they swell and 
soften. Backfilling above the static groundwater table was performed in stages by adding water 
to limited amount of bentonite chips to ensure hydration before additional bentonite chips 
were placed in the borehole. The probe was then pushed through the softened bentonite 
extending to a depth of about 100.1 feet below the existing ground surface using the seismic 
cone penetration testing equipment (SCPT). 
The seismic shear wave velocity test consists of hydraulically pushing a 10-ton steel 
electronic subtraction cone into the bentonite backfilled borehole. The cone has an apex angle 
of 60 degrees and a projected surface area of 1.55 square inches (10 square centimeters) 
equipped with an electronic depth sensor attached to the cone rod. The seismic cone carries a 
uniaxial horizontal geophone to detect the arrival of a shear wave generated and propagated 
from the ground surface. Seismic measurements are made when the SCPT has stopped and a 
shear wave is sent into the subsurface. A shear wave is generated at the surface by striking a 
loaded plank with a switched hammer. The propagation time of the wave from the hammer 
blow to the cone was measured at each discrete depth interval. The vector difference of these 
depths divided by the time difference for the shear wave to arrive at the various depths 
provided the average shear wave velocity over the depth interval. 
The seismic shear wave velocities measured and the average seismic shear wave 
velocity calculated for the top 100 feet of the soil profile at the boring location are presented 
on Plates B-1.1 through B-1.4. The weighted average seismic shear wave velocity for the upper 
100 feet of the soil profile was calculated based on the average shear wave velocity  method 
described in Section 1615.1.5 of the International Building Code (2006 Edition).

A P P E N D I X   C 
Groundwater Level Monitoring 
Groundwater levels were measured during advancement of the boreholes drilled for the 
project at the times shown on the Logs of Borings presented in Appendix A and summarized in 
Tables C-1.1 through C-1.3.   
In addition, groundwater monitoring points were established at two boring locations, 
including B-3(P) and B-11(P), to monitor seasonal fluctuations. The boring locations where 
these groundwater monitoring points are established are shown on the Boring Location Plan, 
Plate 2. 
A total of two vibrating wire piezometers, Model 4500 manufactured by Geokon, Inc., 
were installed at selected depths within the two borings to monitor the groundwater levels on 
a near-continuous basis using a Model LC-2 datalogger manufactured by Geokon, Inc. Catalog 
sheets of the vibrating wire piezometer and datalogger are presented in Figures C-1 and C-2 for 
illustration purposes only. The actual elevations of the vibrating wire piezometers are reflected 
on the installation details prepared for each monitoring point as shown on Plates C-1.1 and 
C-2.1. 
Prior to the installation, the filter stone is saturated by placing the piezometer under 
water a minimum of 12 hours and transported to the field in a submerged condition. In 
addition, zero readings were obtained in the field prior to the installation of each piezometer, 
which provided a baseline reference under field conditions and served as an acceptance test, 
which is a verification that the piezometer is working properly. The piezometers were attached 
to the outside of a 1-inch diameter PVC pipe at the desired depths and the assembly was then 
lowered into the borehole.  The entire borehole with the assembly was then backfilled with a 
cement-bentonite grout up to about 2 feet below the existing ground surface to allow 
placement of the datalogger. 
The groundwater level data are transmitted via a wireless system to cloud data storage 
on a near-continuous basis and processed in our office. Graphic presentations of the 
groundwater level data collected are provided on Plates C-1.2 and C-2.2.

FIGURE C-1 
VIBRATING WIRE PIEZOMETERS 
Standard Piezometers 
Model 4500S(H), 4500AL(V) 
The Model 4500 Standard Piezometer is designed to measure fluid 
pressures such as ground water elevations and pore pressures when 
buried directly in embankments, fills, etc. Also for installation inside 
boreholes, observation wells and standard (>19 mm diameter) 
piezometer riser pipe. The Model 4500SH is designed with a heavy 
duty housing for pressures that exceed 3 MPa. The Model 4500AL is 
designed for low-pressure ranges. The vented version (ALV) provides 
automatic 
compensation 
for 
barometric 
pressure 
changes. 
Thermistors are included to measure temperatures.* 
Specifications 
4500S 
4500SH 
4500AL(V) 
Standard Ranges 
350, 700 kPa; 
1, 2, 3 MPa 
5, 7.5 MPa 
70, 170 kPa 
Over Range 
2 × rated pressure 
Resolution 
0.025% F.S. (minimum) 
Accuracy 
±0.1% F.S. 
Linearity¹ 
<0.5% F.S. (±0.1% F.S. optional) 
Temperature Range² 
−20°C to +80°C

GEOLABS, INC. 
FIGURE C-2 
SINGLE-CHANNEL DATALOGGER 
Single-Channel Dataloggers 
Model 8002-1, 8002-1A (LC-2, LC-2A) 
The Model 8002-1 (LC-2) and Model 8002-1A (LC-2A) are 
stand-alone, single-channel dataloggers, designed to read both the 
vibrating wire element and the integral thermistor of any 
Geokon vibrating wire sensor. The LC-2 offers an internal hard wired 
transducer connection and the LC-2A provides an optional 10-pin 
transducer connector for rapid sensor connection. The dataloggers 
are housed inside a Fiberglass Nema 4X enclosure, are very robust, 
weather-proof and particularly well-suited to operation in harsh 
environments. Power is supplied by two easily accessible ‘D’ cells or 
by solar panel for remote long-term applications. 16,000 arrays can 
be stored (8,000 arrays when used with LogWare) before the oldest 
data is over-written. Strings of LC-2 dataloggers can be daisy 
chained using the RS-485 interface, and versions are available with 
radios and modems to provide remote readout capabilities. Complete 
with Windows® based, user-friendly software. 
Specifications 
Measurement Accuracy 
0.05% F.S. (450-4000Hz) 
Measurement 
Resolution 
1 part in 20,000 
Program Memory 
24K FLASH 
Data Memory 
320K EEPROM 
Temperature Range 
−40°C to +60°C 
L × W × H 
122 × 120 × 91 mm

APPENDIX D  
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
JUNE 2018     Page D-1 
Hawaii  California 
A P P E N D I X   D 
Field Permeability Tests 
The permeability of the in-situ soils were evaluated by performing four constant head and 
four falling head tests in selected borings (Boring Nos. 3, 5, 6, and 7) at depths ranging from 
approximately 16.5 to 22 feet below the existing ground surface. 
The constant head tests were performed by pumping water into the boreholes until the 
water surface has stabilized and remained at equilibrium for a sufficient period of time. The water 
height and the rate of pumping were then measured to provide data upon which to base 
calculation of the subsoil permeability. The test results of the constant head permeability tests are 
presented on Plates D-1 through D-4. 
After completion of the constant head test, the water inside the borehole was allowed to 
drop for the performance of a falling head test.  The time intervals and water drops were recorded 
to provide data upon which to base calculation of the permeability values of the subsoils.  The test 
results of the falling head permeability tests are presented on Plates D-5 through D-8.

Infiltration Tests 
The water infiltration rates into the subsurface soils were tested by performing four 
cased borehole infiltration tests at the bottom of selected borings (Boring Nos. I-1 through I-4) 
in general accordance with the procedures described in the Maryland Stormwater Design 
Manual. Each boring was pre-soaked with water for approximately 2 hours prior to testing. 
Water was introduced into the boring to a depth of about 2 feet above the bottom of the 
boring; and, the drop of the water level in the boring was measured along with respect to time 
for a falling head test consisting of four individual one-hour trials per test. The water level was 
refilled, as necessary, for each trial. The tests were carried out in several increments until 
achieving a steady-state with a relatively constant water infiltration rate. Detailed test data and 
results are presented on Plates E-1 through E-4 in Appendix E. 
W.O. 7384-10
GEOLABS, INC.

APPENDIX F  
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
JUNE 2018     Page F-1 
Hawaii  California 
A P P E N D I X   F 
Laboratory Tests 
Samples recovered from our field exploration program performed for the project were 
visually examined and classified in our laboratory in Honolulu, Hawaii. Selected samples were 
tested to evaluate the physical characteristics and engineering properties of the soils 
encountered. The laboratory testing program was formulated with the emphasis on evaluating 
the moisture, density, plasticity, gradation, shear strength, and corrosivity of the soils 
encountered. 
The laboratory tests performed included determination of dry density and natural 
moisture content, Atterberg Limits, sieve analysis, expansion, unconfined compression, triaxial 
compression, direct shear, CBR, Modified Proctor, and corrosion tests. Results of the tests 
performed are presented on Plates F-1 through F-27. Detailed descriptions of the various tests 
performed for this exploration are presented below. 
Moisture and Density 
Moisture Content and Dry Density tests were performed on selected soil samples to aid 
in defining the interface between the various soil layers encountered and correlating the layers 
between test borings. The moisture content tests were performed in general accordance with 
ASTM D2216. The test results are presented on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample 
depths. 
Atterberg Limits 
Nine (9) Atterberg Limits tests were performed on selected soil samples to evaluate the 
liquid limit (LL), plastic limit (PL), and plasticity index (PI), as an aid in classifying the soils. The 
Atterberg Limits tests were performed in general accordance with ASTM D4318. The test results 
are summarized on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. Graphic presentation 
of the test results are provided on Plate F-1. 
Sieve Analysis and Hydrometer 
To provide information on the particle size distribution of the soils encountered, twenty-
nine (29) Sieve Analysis tests were performed to evaluate the gradation characteristics and to 
aid in soil classification. The tests were performed in accordance with ASTM C117 and C136. 
Graphic presentation of the test results are provided on Plates F-2 through F-7. In addition, 
eight (8) tests were conducted on selected soil samples to evaluate the amount of fines 
content. The test was performed in accordance with ASTM D1140. 
Appendix F 
Laboratory Tests 
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
JUNE 2018     Page F-2 
Hawaii  California 
Ring Swell 
Four (4) one-ring ring swell tests were performed on relatively undisturbed or remolded 
samples to evaluate the swelling potential of the near-surface soils.  The test results are 
summarized on Plate F-8. 
Uniaxial Compressive Strength – Rock Cores 
Ten (10) Uniaxial Compressive Strength tests (ASTM D7012 Method C) were performed 
on selected rock cores to evaluate their unconfined compressive strength of the rock formation 
encountered. Uniaxial compressive strength test results for the selected rock cores are 
presented on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths and presented on Plate F-9. 
Unconsolidated-Undrained Triaxial Compression 
To evaluate the undrained shear strength of the clayey soils, two (2) Unconsolidated 
Undrained Triaxial Compression (TXUU) tests were performed on selected relatively 
undisturbed soil samples in accordance with ASTM D2850. The approximate in-situ effective 
overburden pressure was used as the applied confining pressure for the relatively undisturbed 
samples. The approximate in-situ effective overburden pressure was estimated using the wet 
density of the stratigraphic units and the depth of the groundwater table. The test results and 
the stress-strain curves are presented on Plates F-10 and F-11. 
Direct Shear 
To evaluate the shear strength characteristics of the in-situ soils, five (5) 
strain-controlled, consolidated-drained Direct Shear tests were performed on relatively 
undisturbed soil samples in accordance with ASTM D3080. The test results are presented on 
Plates F-12 through F-16.  
California Bearing Ratio 
Five (5) laboratory California Bearing Ratio tests (ASTM D1883) were performed on bulk 
samples of the near-surface soils to evaluate the pavement support characteristics of the soils. 
The test results are presented on Plates F-17 through F-21. 
Modified Proctor 
Five (5) Modified Proctor compaction tests (ASTM D1557) were performed on bulk 
samples of the near-surface soils to evaluate the dry density and moisture content 
relationships.  The test results are presented on Plates F-22 through F-26. 
Appendix F 
Laboratory Tests 
W.O. 7384-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
JUNE 2018     Page F-3 
Hawaii  California 
Corrosion Potential 
Seven (7) sets of pH (ASTM G51), minimum resistivity (ASTM G57), chloride (EPA 300), 
and sulfate (EPA 300) content tests were performed by our office or TestAmerica Laboratories, 
Inc. on selected soil samples obtained from our field exploration. The test results are presented 
on Plate F-27.

Remolded

A P P E N D I X   H 
Hammer Efficiency Calibration 
Standard penetration test energy measurement calibrations were performed for the 
CME-75DR drill rig equipped with automatic-trip safety sampling hammer used for this project.  
The energy delivered to the sampling rods for the drill rig and hammer combination were 
measured to determine appropriate energy correction factor for the drill rig and hammer 
system. The dynamic testing was performed in 2015 by Geosciences Testing and Research, Inc. 
in general accordance with ASTM D4633, “Standard Test Method for Energy Measurement for 
Dynamic Penetrometers.” Results of the hammer efficiency calibration are presented in 
Appendix H. 
May 21, 2015  
   GTR Project # - 15.146 
Mr. Robin M. Lim, P.E.  
Geolabs, Inc. 
2006 Kalihi Street 
Honolulu, HI 96819 
RE:   Standard Penetration Test Energy Measurement Calibration 
CME-75 DR Truck with Safety Automatic Hammer 
Honolulu, HI 
Dear Robin: 
At your request, we have analyzed the standard penetration test energy measurement 
calibrations taken on May 18, 2015. The testing was conducted in order to evaluate the energy 
delivered to the sampling rods for various drill rig and hammer combinations. This information was 
used to determine the appropriate energy correction factor for the rig-hammer system. The 
particular rig tested in this report was the CME-75 DR equipped with a safety automatic hammer. 
The dynamic testing was carried out in general accordance with ASTM D4633-10, “Standard Test 
Method for Energy Measurement for Dynamic Penetrometers”. 
Field Information 
One green, CME-75 DR drill rig (SN 245327) was evaluated.  The rig was fitted with an 
automatic hammer. Borehole W.O 5537-50 (B-0) was drilled to depths of 1, 3, 5, 10, 15, 20, 25, 30 
and 35 feet to enable the testing.  The drilling was accomplished using 4” OD hollow stem augers 
and AWJ rods. A standard split spoon sampler and a Modified California sampler was used to 
collect the samples. Standard penetration tests (SPTs) were conducted at depths of 1, 3, 5, 10, 15, 
20, 25, 30 and 35 (test numbers #1 through #9) in general accordance with ASTM D1586. The 
length of the rods from the tip of the sampler to the gage locations were 5.3, 8.1, 8.9, 13.8, 18.5, 
23.5, 28.5, 33.5 and 38.5 feet in B-0.  The collected soil samples generally indicated Silty Clay 
(CH) and Sandy Silt (ML).  Refer to Table 1 for details on the field information and conditions. 
Instrumentation 
For tests #1 through #9 the instrumentation consisted of an 18 inch long AWJ rod sub 
section. The rod was equipped with two full bridge foil resistance strain gages. Two piezo resistive 
accelerometer transducers were also attached to each of the instrumented sub sections. A Pile 
Driving AnalyzerTM (PDA) Model 586 PAK, manufactured by Pile Dynamics, Inc. was used to 
collect the data. The PDA is a computer fitted with a data acquisition and signal conditioning 
system.  During driving of the drill string, the strain and acceleration signals are recorded and 
          GEOSCIENCES TESTING AND RESEARCH, INC. 
 55 Middlesex Street, Suite 225, N. Chelmsford, MA  01863 
Ph: (978)251-9395, Fx: (978)251-9396 
GTR                                                                                                                                       
 May 21, 2015 
Project 15.146  
                                                                     Page No. 2 
processed for each hammer blow.  The strain signal is converted to a force record and the 
acceleration signal is converted to a velocity record.  The PDA saves selected hammer blows 
containing this information to disk and determines the compressive stresses, displacement, and 
energy at the point of measurement. This information can be viewed on the computer screen during 
driving.  Refer to Appendix A for literature and background on dynamic testing in general. 
Energy Measurement 
The primary purpose of the testing was to determine the energy transferred from the hammer 
to the instrumented subsection. The PDA measurements of force and velocity were analyzed to 
calculate the energy using the FV method (EMX), which is based on both force and velocity 
measurements as follows: 
EMX = ∫ F(t) v(t)dt 
Another method, known as the EF2 method, was not used due to the limitations associated with the 
measurements (i.e. based on force alone assuming proportionality at the gage locations since 
velocity is not used in the analysis). The EF2 method is not the recommended method in ASTM 
D4633. 
The rated energy (designated as ER) for the hammer is 0.35 kip-ft based on the 2.5 feet (30 
inch) drop height and 0.14 kip (140 pound) ram. The actual efficiency or energy transfer ratio 
(ETR) for the hammer system can then be determined using EMX and ER as follows: 
ETR = (EMX/ER)*100% 
For SPT, the typical ETR is taken as 60%, based on history and experience from prior studies. To 
adjust the measured SPT N values (Nmeasured) for actual hammer performance, the ETR can be 
used to correct the Nmeasured to N60 by using the correction factor (Cn) determined as: 
Cn = ETR/60%  
and N60 = Cn * Nmeasured.  
This is a correction for energy only. The application of additional corrections for overburden, non 
conventional samplers, etc., was not considered in this study and should be evaluated for 
appropriateness by the geotechnical engineer for this project. 
Results 
The results of the testing program are summarized in Tables 1 for the safety automatic 
hammer. The tables include EMX, ER, ETR, FMX (maximum measured force in the rod 
subsection), and BPM (blows per minute). The blow count, including the SPT N- value, and the soil 
description were recorded by others.  The data was presented with statistical information including 
the average, maximum, minimum and standard deviation for the N-value associated with group of 
data sets (sample depths) for the hammer. In addition, the data for all depths for a particular hammer 
combined are also presented in the tables.  Note that the N-value may not match the number of 
GTR                                                                                                                                       
 May 21, 2015 
Project 15.146  
                                                                     Page No. 3 
blows analyzed due to differences between the inspector’s blow count observations and the PDA 
recorded number.  In some cases, bad blows or unreliable data from the PDA may also have been 
omitted. The results for the blows are presented in Appendix B as plots with blow number and in 
tabular form.  
For the CME-75 DR (truck) rig equipped with the safety automatic hammer, the results 
indicate that the average ETR varied between 70% and 81% for the nine depths. The average ETR 
for all data was around 74%, resulting in an overall Cn = 1.24.  The typical blow rate was around 53 
bpm for the automatic hammer. 
This report has been prepared in accordance with generally accepted geotechnical 
engineering principles with specific application to this project. Our conclusions are based on 
applicable standards of practice, including any information reported to and/or prepared for us.  No 
other warranty, expressed or implied, is made.  
We have appreciated this opportunity to work with you on this project.  If you have any 
questions regarding this report, please contact us at (978) 251-9395. 
Sincerely, 
Geosciences Testing and Research, Inc. 
William D. Oder

Sandy Silt (ML)

11
1.35
2. The soil description and SPT N-value were recorded by others. The SPT N-value is the sum of the middle 2 numbers when the sampler s driven for 4 - six inch intervals or 
    the sum of the last 2 numbers when the sampler is driven for 3 - six inch inervals.
3. Blows analyzed correspond to SPT N-value and may not match up exactly with the N-value due to differences in blow count logging between PDA and inspector or poor data quality.
4. EMX is the integration of F and V obtained from the PDA.
5. ER is the rated energy of 0.35 kip-ft based on 140 pound hammer and 2.5 feet drop height.
6. ETR is the energy transfer ratio based on (EMX/ER)*100%.
7. Cn is the energy correction factor which is equal to ETR/60% and is used to convert the measured SPT N-value to the corrected equivalent value representing 60% energy transfer.
APPENDIX A 
DYNAMIC ANALYSIS LITERATURE  
GTR HSDPT Method 
rev. 1/2012 
HIGH STRAIN DYNAMIC PILE TESTING 
Introduction 
Dynamic pile testing (a.k.a. High Strain Dynamic Pile Testing - HSDPT) is commonly 
employed for evaluating the capacity of driven piles. It is also provides information about hammer 
performance and pile integrity/stresses. Dynamic testing is carried out in accordance with ASTM 
D4945, “Standard Test Method for High Strain Dynamic Testing of Piles”. Dynamic pile testing 
involves using strain gages and accelerometers to record an impact wave and its reflections generated 
by a piling hammer. Both driven piles and drilled foundations can be tested (provided that an impact 
hammer is used to create the high strain wave for the drilled foundations).   
Procedure 
Dynamic pile testing was performed using a Pile Driving Analyzer (PDA®), such as the PAK®, 
PAL®, or PAX® systems, manufactured by Pile Dynamics, Inc. (PDI) of Cleveland, Ohio. These 
systems are computers fitted with data acquisition and signal conditioning components. The 
instrumentation consists of two strain gages and two accelerometer transducers attached a minimum of 
1.5 pile diameters below the pile top.  During impact, the strain and acceleration signals are recorded 
and processed for each hammer blow.  The strain signal is converted to a force record and the 
acceleration signal is converted to a velocity record.  The PDA® saves selected hammer blows 
containing this information to disk and determines the transferred energy, compressive/tensile stresses, 
displacement, pile integrity, and the estimated pile bearing capacity using the Case Method.  This 
information can be viewed on the computer screen during driving.  A screen shot of data collection in 
the PDA® Windows (PDA-W®) Program is provided in Figure 1.  Selected blows can be further 
processed to predict the static pile capacity using signal matching programs.   
Figure 1. Data collection during pile driving in the (PDI - PDA®-Win Program). 
GTR HSDPT Method 
rev. 1/2012 
Theory 
When a ram strikes the pile head, it initiates a large strain wave that propagates down the pile 
as illustrated in Figure 2.  External soil resistance or changes in the pile’s impedance (due to variations 
in the pile’s material or geometry) causes reflection waves that are recorded by the instrumentation.    
Knowing the material properties and pile geometry at the point of measurement, the strain can be 
converted to force, while the acceleration is integrated with time to produce velocity.   
Figure 2. Pile instrumentation and hammer impact. 
As long as there is no change in the pile impedance and there are no external forces (i.e. 
friction), the force and velocity are proportional (equal).  Reflections at the tip can be explained by two 
classical boundary conditions.  Free end conditions (analogous to easy driving through soft clay) 
require zero force and no velocity restrictions at the tip, resulting in a compression wave returning as a 
tension wave and an increase in velocity (theoretically doubling).  Figure 3 graphically presents a 
typical reflection from a pipe pile during penetration into soft clay. Fixed end conditions (analogous to 
hard driving into bedrock) require zero velocity and no force restrictions at the tip, resulting in a 
compression wave being reflected with a greater magnitude than the incident wave (theoretically 
doubling) and the tip velocity at theoretically zero. Figure 4 graphically presents a typical reflection 
from an H-pile driven to bedrock.  The time the wave takes to travel down to the tip and reflect back to 
the transducers is twice the pile length divided by the wave speed of the pile material (2L/C). 
Accelerometer
Strain Gage 
RAM
GTR HSDPT Method 
rev. 1/2012 
Figure 3. Typical Force and Velocity traces for a pipe pile driven into soft clay 
 (high velocity and low force at tip - 2L/C).  
Figure 4. Typical Force and Velocity traces for an H-pile driven into bedrock 
(high force and low velocity at tip - 2L/C).  
GTR HSDPT Method 
rev. 1/2012 
If a pile contains a defect or is damaged (e.g. reduction in impedance) during driving, the wave 
reflecting from the zone of decreased impedance will show a reduction in the force and increase in the 
velocity (somewhat comparable to “free end conditions”). These reflections would arrive to the 
measuring transducers before the expected reflections associated with the pile tip as the damaged zone 
is at a point along the pile between the transducer location and pile tip.  The detection of damage 
during driving is usually easily identifiable and typically associated with cracking of concrete piles or 
splice breakage.      
Dynamic Testing Summary Output 
After data collection, the most pertinent output quantities from the dynamic pile testing can be 
summarized in a graphical manner.  The data can be also presented in tabular format, averaging the 
results based on penetration depth or blow number as specified by the user.  Figure 5 shows typical 
graphical output. Each of the three plots presents two quantities sharing the vertical (penetration) axis.        
Figure 5. Typical Dynamic Testing summary Output (PDI Plot® Program)  
Signal Matching Analyses 
Signal matching using the dynamic testing data can be performed to predict the static pile 
capacity.  Programs such as CAPWAP® (developed by Pile Dynamics, Inc.) or TEPWAP/PWAP 
(developed by GTR) are numerical analyses used to solve the one dimensional wave equation using the 
measured force and velocity. E.A Smith (1960) suggested modeling the hammer-pile-soil system for use 
in the wave equation by a series of masses, springs and dashpots as shown in Figure 6.   The signal 
matching programs determine the best match between measured and calculated pile top forces and 
GTR HSDPT Method 
rev. 1/2012 
replace the hammer input with the measured force and velocity. The pile is separated into many small 
segments, often 1 meter in length.  The velocity record obtained from the dynamic pile testing 
transducers is used as input to the top pile segment. The resistance, damping, and quake are the 
primary soil parameters assigned by the user to each pile segment below grade. The signal matching 
programs will calculate the displacement, velocity, and stresses (forces) for each pile segment based on 
the input velocity record and the user assigned soil parameters.   These parameters are adjusted and 
modified in an iterative fashion until the best match is obtained between the force calculated for the 
pile top segment and the force measured at the pile top during testing.  The user assigned soil 
parameters based on the best match represent the “actual soil conditions”, including the resistance (and 
therefore pile capacity). This capacity is based on the resistance at the time of the testing.  Static load 
tests are typically conducted several days or weeks after driving.  Therefore, restrike tests are 
recommended to be performed some time after driving to assess time dependent changes in pile 
capacity, such as setup or relaxation.  
New PDA Appendix.docx 
Figure 6. Signal Matching Model (i.e. CAPWAP® or TEPWAP/PWAP).
